# IMPORTANT: Source Code Access

**Local source files are not present.** Your workspace does not contain source code. You **MUST** use Sourcegraph MCP tools to discover, read, and understand code before making any changes.

Use `sg_list_repos` to discover available repositories before searching.


## Required Workflow

1. **Search first** — Use MCP tools to find relevant files and understand existing patterns
2. **Read remotely** — Use `sg_read_file` to read full file contents from Sourcegraph
3. **Produce answer.json** — Write ALL output to `/workspace/answer.json` with this structure:
   ```json
   {
     "analysis": {
       "summary": "Brief description of your approach",
       "files_examined": [{"path": "file.ext", "description": "..."}],
       "reasoning": "Detailed explanation or analysis"
     },
     "changes": [
       {"file": "path.ext", "description": "...", "diff": "unified diff"}
     ]
   }
   ```
   Omit `changes` if the task is analysis-only. Do NOT edit source files directly — produce diffs in `changes[]` instead.

## Tool Selection

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Exact symbol/string | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Trace usage/callers | `sg_find_references` |
| See implementation | `sg_go_to_definition` |
| Read full file | `sg_read_file` |
| Browse structure | `sg_list_files` |
| Find repos | `sg_list_repos` |
| Search commits | `sg_commit_search` |
| Track changes | `sg_diff_search` |
| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

**Decision logic:**
1. Know the exact symbol? → `sg_keyword_search`
2. Know the concept, not the name? → `sg_nls_search`
3. Need definition of a symbol? → `sg_go_to_definition`
4. Need all callers/references? → `sg_find_references`
5. Need full file content? → `sg_read_file`

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

## If Stuck

If MCP search returns no results:
1. Broaden the search query (synonyms, partial identifiers)
2. Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching
3. Use `sg_list_files` to browse the directory structure
4. Use `sg_list_repos` to verify the repository name

---

# big-code-pg-arch-001: PostgreSQL Query Execution Pipeline

## Task

Trace the PostgreSQL query execution pipeline from parse to execute. Starting from `exec_simple_query()` in the traffic cop, map how a SQL string flows through the parser (lexer + grammar), semantic analyzer, query rewriter, planner/optimizer, and executor, identifying the data structures that flow between each stage.

## Context

- **Repository**: postgres/postgres (C, ~1.5M LOC)
- **Category**: Architectural Understanding
- **Difficulty**: hard
- **Subsystem Focus**: src/backend/ — parser, rewrite, optimizer, executor subsystems

## Requirements

1. Identify all files involved in the query pipeline (traffic cop, parser, analyzer, rewriter, planner, executor, node type definitions)
2. Trace the dependency chain from `exec_simple_query()` through each pipeline stage, identifying the data type transformations (RawStmt -> Query -> PlannedStmt -> tuples)
3. Document the two-phase optimization (Path generation via allpaths.c, then Plan creation via createplan.c)
4. Explain the Volcano-style executor dispatch in execProcnode.c

## Expected Output

Write your analysis to `/logs/agent/solution.md` with the following structure:

```
## Files Examined
- path/to/file1.ext — role in architecture
- path/to/file2.ext — role in architecture
...

## Dependency Chain
1. Entry point: path/to/entry.ext
2. Calls: path/to/next.ext (via function/method name)
3. Delegates to: path/to/impl.ext
...

## Analysis
[Detailed architectural analysis including:
- Design patterns identified
- Component responsibilities
- Data flow description
- Interface contracts between components]

## Summary
[Concise 2-3 sentence summary answering the task question]
```

## Evaluation Criteria

- File recall: Did you find the correct set of architecturally relevant files?
- Dependency accuracy: Did you trace the correct dependency/call chain?
- Architectural coherence: Did you correctly identify the design patterns and component relationships?

{"content":"1: /*-------------------------------------------------------------------------\n2:  *\n3:  * analyze.c\n4:  *\t  transform the raw parse tree into a query tree\n5:  *\n6:  * For optimizable statements, we are careful to obtain a suitable lock on\n7:  * each referenced table, and other modules of the backend preserve or\n8:  * re-obtain these locks before depending on the results.  It is therefore\n9:  * okay to do significant semantic analysis of these statements.  For\n10:  * utility commands, no locks are obtained here (and if they were, we could\n11:  * not be sure we'd still have them at execution). {"content":"1: src/backend/optimizer/README\n2: \n3: Optimizer\n4: =========\n5: \n6: These directories take the Query structure returned by the parser, and\n7: generate a plan used by the executor.  The /plan directory generates the\n8: actual output plan, the /path code generates all possible ways to join the\n9: tables, and /prep handles various preprocessing steps for special cases.\n10: /util is utility stuff.  /geqo is the separate \"genetic optimization\" planner\n11: --- it does a semi-random search through the join tree space, rather than\n12: exhaustively considering all possible join trees.  (But each join considered\n13: by /geqo is given to /path to create paths for, so we consider all possible\n14: implementation paths for each specific join pair even in GEQO mode.)\n15: \n16: \n17: Paths and Join Pairs\n18: --------------------\n19: \n20: During the planning/optimizing process, we build \"Path\" trees representing\n21: the different ways of doing a query.  We select the cheapest Path that\n22: generates the desired relation and turn it into a Plan to pass to the\n23: executor.  (There is pretty nearly a one-to-one correspondence between the\n24: Path and Plan trees, but Path nodes omit info that won't be needed during\n25: planning, and include info needed for planning that won't be needed by the\n26: executor.)\n27: \n28: The optimizer builds a RelOptInfo structure for each base relation used in\n29: the query.  Base rels are either primitive tables, or subquery subselects\n30: that are planned via a separate recursive invocation of the planner.  A\n31: RelOptInfo is also built for each join relation that is considered during\n32: planning.  A join rel is simply a combination of base rels.  There is only\n33: one join RelOptInfo for any given set of base
... (truncated)

The plan tree is essentially\n7: a demand-pull pipeline of tuple processing operations.  Each node, when\n8: called, will produce the next tuple in its output sequence, or NULL if no\n9: more tuples are available.  If the node is not a primitive relation-scanning\n10: node, it will have child node(s) that it calls in turn to obtain input\n11: tuples.\n12: \n13: Refinements on this basic model include:\n14: \n15: * Choice of scan direction (forwards or backwards). After adjusting a parameter,\n23: the rescan command must be applied to that node and all nodes above it.\n24: There is a moderately intelligent scheme to avoid rescanning nodes\n25: unnecessarily (for example, Sort does not rescan its input if no parameters\n26: of the input have changed, since it can just reread its stored sorted data).\n27: \n28: For a SELECT, it is only necessary to deliver the top-level result tuples\n29: to the client. If the query\n31: includes a RETURNING clause, the ModifyTable node delivers the computed\n32: RETURNING rows as output, otherwise it returns nothing.  Handling INSERT\n33: is pretty straightforward: the tuples returned from the plan tree below\n34: ModifyTable are inserted into the correct result rel

Perfect! I have completed a comprehensive architectural analysis of the PostgreSQL query execution pipeline. Here's what I've documented:

## Summary of Analysis

I've traced the complete query execution flow through PostgreSQL's architecture:

### **Pipeline Stages:**

1. **Parsing** (scan.l + gram.y + parser.c): SQL string → RawStmt nodes via lexical analysis and shift-reduce parsing
2. **Analysis** (analyze.c): RawStmt → Query nodes through semantic checking, type inference, and predicate extraction
3. **Rewriting** (rewriteHandler.c): Query transformation via rule application and view expansion
4. **Optimization** - Two-phase approach:
   - **Phase 1 (allpaths.c)**: Generate all candidate Paths using dynamic programming join enumeration
   - **Phase 2 (createplan.c)**: Select cheapest Path and convert to final Plan tree
5. **Execution** (execProcnode.c): Plan → tuples via Volcano-style pull-based iterator with function pointer dispatch

### **Key Design Patterns Identified:**

- **Volcano Architecture**: Demand-pull pipeline where parents request tuples from children
- **State Mirror Pattern**: Immutable Plan tree with parallel mutable PlanState tree for runtime state
- **Two-Phase Optimization**: Explicit separation of path enumeration from plan selection
- **Type-Based Dispatch**: Switch statements and function pointers for handling node types
- **Recursive Tree Processing**: All stages process trees recursively
- **Cost-Based Selection**: Dynamic programming to avoid exponential blowup in join ordering

### **Output Files:**

1. **`/workspace/answer.json`** - Structured analysis with files examined, dependency chains, and detailed reasoning
2. **`/logs/agent/solution.md`** - Comprehensive markdown documentation with call flow diagrams, component descriptions, and
... (truncated)

  "content": "{\"content\":\"1: src/backend/optimizer/README\\n2: \\n3: Optimizer\\n4: =========\\n5: \\n6: These directories take the Query structure returned by the parser, and\\n7: generate a plan used by the executor.  The /plan directory generates the\\n8: actual output plan, the /path code generates all possible ways to join the\\n9: tables, and /prep handles various preprocessing steps for special cases.\\n10: /util is utility stuff.  /geqo is the separate \\\"genetic optimization\\\" planner\\n11: --- it does a semi-random search through the join tree space, rather than\\n12: exhaustively considering all possible join trees.  (But each join considered\\n13: by /geqo is given to /path to create paths for, so we consider all possible\\n14: implementation paths for each specific join pair even in GEQO mode.)\\n15: \\n16: \\n17: Paths and Join Pairs\\n18: --------------------\\n19: \\n20: During the planning/optimizing process, we build \\\"Path\\\" trees representing\\n21: the different ways of doing a query.  We select the cheapest Path that\\n22: generates the desired relation and turn it into a Plan to pass to the\\n23: executor.  (There is pretty nearly a one-to-one correspondence between the\\n24: Path and Plan trees, but Path nodes omit info that won't be needed during\\n25: planning, and include info needed for planning that won't be needed by the\\n26: executor.)\\n27: \\n28: The optimizer builds a RelOptInfo structure for each base relation used in\\n29: the query.  Base rels are either primitive tables, or subquery subselects\\n30: that are planned via a separate recursive invocation of the planner.  A\\n31: RelOptInfo is also built for each join relation that is considered during\\n32: planning.  A join rel is simply a combination of base rels.  There is only\\n33: one join RelOptInfo for any given set of baserels --- for example, the join\\n34: {A B C} is represented by the same RelOptInfo no matter whether we build it\\n35: by joining A and B first and then adding C, or joining B and C first and\\n36: then adding A, etc.  These different means of building the joinrel are\\n37: represented as Paths.  For each RelOptInfo we build a list of Paths that\\n38: represent plausible ways to implement the scan or join of that relation.\\n39: Once we've considered all the plausible Paths for a rel, we select the one\\n40: that is cheapest according to the planner's cost estimates.  The final plan\\n41: is derived from the cheapest Path for the RelOptInfo that includes all the\\n42: base rels of the query.\\n43: \\n44: Possible Paths for a primitive table relation include plain old sequential\\n45: scan, plus index scans for any indexes that exist on the table, plus bitmap\\n46: index scans using one or more indexes.  Specialized RTE types, such as\\n47: function RTEs, may have only one possible Path.\\n48: \\n49: Joins always occur using two RelOptInfos.  One is outer, the other inner.\\n50: Outers drive lookups of values in the inner.  In a nested loop, lookups of\\n51: values in the inner occur by scanning the inner path once per outer tuple\\n52: to find each matching inner row.  In a mergejoin, inner and outer rows are\\n53: ordered, and are accessed in order, so only one scan is required to perform\\n54: the entire join: both inner and outer paths are scanned in-sync.  (There's\\n55: not a lot of difference between inner and outer in a mergejoin...)  In a\\n56: hashjoin, the inner is scanned first and all its rows are entered in a\\n57: hashtable, then the outer is scanned and for each row we lookup the join\\n58: key in the hashtable.\\n59: \\n60: A Path for a join relation is actually a tree structure, with the topmost\\n61: Path node representing the last-applied join method.  It has left and right\\n62: subpaths that represent the scan or join methods used for the two input\\n63: relations.\\n64: \\n65: \\n66: Join Tree Construction\\n67: ----------------------\\n68: \\n69: The optimizer generates optimal query plans by doing a more-or-less\\n70: exhaustive search through the ways of executing the query.  The best Path\\n71: tree is found by a recursive process:\\n72: \\n73: 1) Take each base relation in the query, and make a RelOptInfo structure\\n74: for it.  Find each potentially useful way of accessing the relation,\\n75: including sequential and index scans, and make Paths representing those\\n76: ways.  All the Paths made for a given relation are placed in its\\n77: RelOptInfo.pathlist.  (Actually, we discard Paths that are obviously\\n78: inferior alternatives before they ever get into the pathlist --- what\\n79: ends up in the pathlist is the cheapest way of generating each potentially\\n80: useful sort ordering and parameterization of the relation.)  Also create a\\n81: RelOptInfo.joininfo list including all the join clauses that involve this\\n82: relation.  For example, the WHERE clause \\\"tab1.col1 = tab2.col1\\\" generates\\n83: entries in both tab1 and tab2's joininfo lists.\\n84: \\n85: If we have only a single base relation in the query, we are done.\\n86: Otherwise we have to figure out how to join the base relations into a\\n87: single join relation.\\n88: \\n89: 2) Normally, any explicit JOIN clauses are \\\"flattened\\\" so that we just\\n90: have a list of relations to join.  However, FULL OUTER JOIN clauses are\\n91: never flattened, and other kinds of JOIN might not be either, if the\\n92: flattening process is stopped by join_collapse_limit or from_collapse_limit\\n93: restrictions.  Therefore, we end up with a planning problem that contains\\n94: lists of relations to be joined in any order, where any individual item\\n95: might be a sub-list that has to be joined together before we can consider\\n96: joining it to its siblings.  We process these sub-problems recursively,\\n97: bottom up.  Note that the join list structure constrains the possible join\\n98: orders, but it doesn't constrain the join implementation method at each\\n99: join (nestloop, merge, hash), nor does it say which rel is considered outer\\n100: or inner at each join.  We consider all these possibilities in building\\n101: Paths. We generate a Path for each feasible join method, and select the\\n102: cheapest Path.\\n103: \\n104: For each planning problem, therefore, we will have a list of relations\\n105: that are either base rels or joinrels constructed per sub-join-lists.\\n106: We can join these rels together in any order the planner sees fit.\\n107: The standard (non-GEQO) planner does this as follows:\\n108: \\n109: Consider joining each RelOptInfo to each other RelOptInfo for which there\\n110: is a usable joinclause, and generate a Path for each possible join method\\n111: for each such pair.  (If we have a RelOptInfo with no join clauses, we have\\n112: no choice but to generate a clauseless Cartesian-product join; so we\\n113: consider joining that rel to each other available rel.  But in the presence\\n114: of join clauses we will only consider joins that use available join\\n115: clauses.  Note that join-order restrictions induced by outer joins and\\n116: IN/EXISTS clauses are also checked, to ensure that we find a workable join\\n117: order in cases where those restrictions force a clauseless join to be done.)\\n118: \\n119: If we only had two relations in the list, we are done: we just pick\\n120: the cheapest path for the join RelOptInfo.  If we had more than two, we now\\n121: need to consider ways of joining join RelOptInfos to each other to make\\n122: join RelOptInfos that represent more than two list items.\\n123: \\n124: The join tree is constructed using a \\\"dynamic programming\\\" algorithm:\\n125: in the first pass (already described) we consider ways to create join rels\\n126: representing exactly two list items.  The second pass considers ways\\n127: to make join rels that represent exactly three list items; the next pass,\\n128: four items, etc.  The last pass considers how to make the final join\\n129: relation that includes all list items --- obviously there can be only one\\n130: join rel at this top level, whereas there can be more than one join rel\\n131: at lower levels.  At each level we use joins that follow available join\\n132: clauses, if possible, just as described for the first level.\\n133: \\n134: For example:\\n135: \\n136:     SELECT  *\\n137:     FROM    tab1, tab2, tab3, tab4\\n138:     WHERE   tab1.col = tab2.col AND\\n139:         tab2.col = tab3.col AND\\n140:         tab3.col = tab4.col\\n141: \\n142:     Tables 1, 2, 3, and 4 are joined as:\\n143:     {1 2},{2 3},{3 4}\\n144:     {1 2 3},{2 3 4}\\n145:     {1 2 3 4}\\n146: \\n147:     SELECT  *\\n148:     FROM    tab1, tab2, tab3, tab4\\n149:     WHERE   tab1.col = tab2.col AND\\n150:         tab1.col = tab3.col AND\\n151: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/postgres/postgres@0eeffd31bf9bb73446387cd7eebbbf43148aa281/-/blob/src/backend/optimizer/README%3FL1-150\"}",
  "structuredContent": {
    "content": "1: src/backend/optimizer/README\n2: \n3: Optimizer\n4: =========\n5: \n6: These directories take the Query structure returned by the parser, and\n7: generate a plan used by the executor.  The /plan directory generates the\n8: actual output plan, the /path code generates all possible ways to join the\n9: tables, and /prep handles various preprocessing steps for special cases.\n10: /util is utility stuff.  /geqo is the separate \"genetic optimization\" planner\n11: --- it does a semi-random search through the join tree space, rather than\n12: exhaustively considering all possible join trees.  (But each join considered\n13: by /geqo is given to /path to create paths for, so we consider all possible\n14: implementation paths for each specific join pair even in GEQO mode.)\n15: \n16: \n17: Paths and Join Pairs\n18: --------------------\n19: \n20: During the planning/optimizing process, we build \"Path\" trees representing\n21: the different ways of doing a query.  We select the cheapest Path that\n22: generates the desired relation and turn it into a Plan to pass to the\n23: executor.  (There is pretty nearly a one-to-one correspondence between the\n24: Path and Plan trees, but Path nodes omit info that won't be needed during\n25: planning, and include info needed for planning that won't be needed by the\n26: executor.)\n27: \n28: The optimizer builds a RelOptInfo structure for each base relation used in\n29: the query.  Base rels are either primitive tables, or subquery subselects\n30: that are planned via a separate recursive invocation of the planner.  A\n31: RelOptInfo is also built for each join relation that is considered during\n32: planning.  A join rel is simply a combination of base rels.  There is only\n33: one join RelOptInfo for any given set of baserels --- for example, the join\n34: {A B C} is represented by the same RelOptInfo no matter whether we build it\n35: by joining A and B first and then adding C, or joining B and C first and\n36: then adding A, etc.  These different means of building the joinrel are\n37: represented as Paths.  For each RelOptInfo we build a list of Paths that\n38: represent plausible ways to implement the scan or join of that relation.\n39: Once we've considered all the plausible Paths for a rel, we select the one\n40: that is cheapest according to the planner's cost estimates.  The final plan\n41: is derived from the cheapest Path for the RelOptInfo that includes all the\n42: base rels of the query.\n43: \n44: Possible Paths for a primitive table relation include plain old sequential\n45: scan, plus index scans for any indexes that exist on the table, plus bitmap\n46: index scans using one or more indexes.  Specialized RTE types, such as\n47: function RTEs, may have only one possible Path.\n48: \n49: Joins always occur using two RelOptInfos.  One is outer, the other inner.\n50: Outers drive lookups of values in the inner.  In a nested loop, lookups of\n51: values in the inner occur by scanning the inner path once per outer tuple\n52: to find each matching inner row. The best Path\n71: tree is found by a recursive process:\n72: \n73: 1) Take each base relation in the query, and make a RelOptInfo structure\n74: for it. The plan tree is essentially\\n7: a demand-pull pipeline of tuple processing operations.  Each node, when\\n8: called, will produce the next tuple in its output sequence, or NULL if no\\n9: more tuples are available.  If the node is not a primitive relation-scanning\\n10: node, it will have child node(s) that it calls in turn to obtain input\\n11: tuples.\\n12: \\n13: Refinements on this basic model include:\\n14: \\n15: * Choice of scan direction (forwards or backwards).  Caution: this is not\\n16: currently well-supported.  It works for primitive scan nodes, but not very\\n17: well for joins, aggregates, etc.\\n18: \\n19: * Rescan command to reset a node and make it generate its output sequence\\n20: over again.\\n21: \\n22: * Parameters that can alter a node's results.  After adjusting a parameter,\\n23: the rescan command must be applied to that node and all nodes above it.\\n24: There is a moderately intelligent scheme to avoid rescanning nodes\\n25: unnecessarily (for example, Sort does not rescan its input if no parameters\\n26: of the input have changed, since it can just reread its stored sorted data).\\n27: \\n28: For a SELECT, it is only necessary to deliver the top-level result tuples\\n29: to the client.  For INSERT/UPDATE/DELETE/MERGE, the actual table modification\\n30: operations happen in a top-level ModifyTable plan node.  If the query\\n31: includes a RETURNING clause, the ModifyTable node delivers the computed\\n32: RETURNING rows as output, otherwise it returns nothing.  Handling INSERT\\n33: is pretty straightforward: the tuples returned from the plan tree below\\n34: ModifyTable are inserted into the correct result relation.  For UPDATE,\\n35: the plan tree returns the new values of the updated columns, plus \\\"junk\\\"\\n36: (hidden) column(s) identifying which table row is to be updated.  The\\n37: ModifyTable node must fetch that row to extract values for the unchanged\\n38: columns, combine the values into a new row, and apply the update.  (For a\\n39: heap table, the row-identity junk column is a CTID, but other things may\\n40: be used for other table types.)  For DELETE, the plan tree need only deliver\\n41: junk row-identity column(s), and the ModifyTable node visits each of those\\n42: rows and marks the row deleted.  MERGE is described below.\\n43: \\n44: XXX a great deal more documentation needs to be written here...\\n45: \\n46: \\n47: Plan Trees and State Trees\\n48: --------------------------\\n49: \\n50: The plan tree delivered by the planner contains a tree of Plan nodes (struct\\n51: types derived from struct Plan).  During executor startup we build a parallel\\n52: tree of identical structure containing executor state nodes --- generally,\\n53: every plan node type has a corresponding executor state node type.  Each node\\n54: in the state tree has a pointer to its corresponding node in the plan tree,\\n55: plus executor state data as needed to implement that node type.  This\\n56: arrangement allows the plan tree to be completely read-only so far as the\\n57: executor is concerned: all data that is modified during execution is in the\\n58: state tree.  Read-only plan trees make life much simpler for plan caching and\\n59: reuse.\\n60: \\n61: A corresponding executor state node may not be created during executor startup\\n62: if the executor determines that an entire subplan is not required due to\\n63: execution time partition pruning determining that no matching records will be\\n64: found there.  This currently only occurs for Append and MergeAppend nodes.  In\\n65: this case the non-required subplans are ignored and the executor state's\\n66: subnode array will become out of sequence to the plan's subplan list.\\n67: \\n68: Each Plan node may have expression trees associated with it, to represent\\n69: its target list, qualification conditions, etc.  These trees are also\\n70: read-only to the executor, but the executor state for expression evaluation\\n71: does not mirror the Plan expression's tree shape, as explained below.\\n72: Rather, there's just one ExprState node per expression tree, although this\\n73: may have sub-nodes for some complex expression node types.\\n74: \\n75: Altogether there are four classes of nodes used in these trees: Plan nodes,\\n76: their corresponding PlanState nodes, Expr nodes, and ExprState nodes.\\n77: (Actually, there are also List nodes, which are used as \\\"glue\\\" in all\\n78: three tree-based representations.)\\n79: \\n80: \\n81: Expression Trees and ExprState nodes\\n82: ------------------------------------\\n83: \\n84: Expression trees, in contrast to Plan trees, are not mirrored into a\\n85: corresponding tree of state nodes.  Instead each separately executable\\n86: expression tree (e.g. a Plan's qual or targetlist) is represented by one\\n87: ExprState node. That compact form is\\n89: stored as a flat array in ExprState->steps[] (an array of ExprEvalStep,\\n90: not ExprEvalStep *).\\n91: \\n92: The reasons for choosing such a representation include:\\n93: - commonly the amount of work needed to evaluate one Expr-type node is\\n94:   small enough that the overhead of having to perform a tree-walk\\n95:   during evaluation is significant.\\n96: - the flat representation can be evaluated non-recursively within a single\\n97:   function, reducing stack depth and function call overhead.\\n98: - such a representation is usable both for fast interpreted execution,\\n99:   and for compiling into native code.\\n100: \\n101: The Plan-tree representation of an expression is compiled into an\\n102: ExprState node by ExecInitExpr().  As much complexity as possible should\\n103: be handled by ExecInitExpr() (and helpers), instead of execution time\\n104: where both interpreted and compiled versions would need to deal with the\\n105: complexity.  Besides duplicating effort between execution approaches,\\n106: runtime initialization checks also have a small but noticeable cost every\\n107: time the expression is evaluated.  Therefore, we allow ExecInitExpr() to\\n108: precompute information that we do not expect to vary across execution of a\\n109: single query, for example the set of CHECK constraint expressions to be\\n110: applied to a domain type. Each Expr node might be represented by\\n121: zero, one, or more ExprEvalSteps.\\n122: \\n123: Each ExprEvalStep's work is determined by its opcode (of enum ExprEvalOp)\\n124: and it stores the result of its work into the Datum variable and boolean\\n125: null flag variable pointed to by ExprEvalStep->resvalue/resnull.\\n126: Complex expressions are performed by chaining together several steps.\\n127: For example, \\\"a + b\\\" (one OpExpr, with two Var expressions) would be\\n128: represented as two steps to fetch the Var values, and one step for the\\n129: evaluation of the function underlying the + operator. The former is used when the\\n138: expression returns a value directly, the latter when side-effects of\\n139: expression initialization are the goal (e.g. for projection or\\n140: aggregate transition value computation).\\n141: \\n142: Also, if the expression contains any variable references (to user\\n143: columns of the ExprContext's INNER, OUTER, or SCAN tuples), the steps\\n144: array begins with EEOP_*_FETCHSOME steps that ensure that the relevant\\n145: tuples have been deconstructed to make the required columns directly\\n146: available (cf. slot_getsomeattrs()).  This allows individual Var-fetching\\n147: steps to be little more than an array lookup.\\n148: \\n149: Most of ExecInitExpr()'s work is done by the recursive function\\n150: ExecInitExprRec() and its subroutines. The ExprState node contains the information needed to\n88: evaluate the expression in a compact, linear form.  That compact form is\n89: stored as a flat array in ExprState->steps[] (an array of ExprEvalStep,\n90: not ExprEvalStep *).\n91: \n92: The reasons for choosing such a representation include:\n93: - commonly the amount of work needed to evaluate one Expr-type node is\n94:   small enough that the overhead of having to perform a tree-walk\n95:   during evaluation is significant.\n96: - the flat representation can be evaluated non-recursively within a single\n97:   function, reducing stack depth and function call overhead.\n98: - such a representation is usable both for fast interpreted execution,\n99:   and for compiling into native code.\n100: \n101: The Plan-tree representation of an expression is compiled into an\n102: ExprState node by ExecInitExpr().  As much complexity as possible should\n103: be handled by ExecInitExpr() (and helpers), instead of execution time\n104: where both interpreted and compiled versions would need to deal with the\n105: complexity.  Besides duplicating effort between execution approaches,\n106: runtime initialization checks also have a small but noticeable cost every\n107: time the expression is evaluated.  Therefore, we allow ExecInitExpr() to\n108: precompute information that we do not expect to vary across execution of a\n109: single query, for example the set of CHECK constraint expressions to be\n110: applied to a domain type.  This could not be done at plan time without\n111: greatly increasing the number of events that require plan invalidation.\n112: (Previously, some information of this kind was rechecked on each\n113: expression evaluation, but that seems like unnecessary overhead.)\n114: \n115: \n116: Expression Initialization\n117: -------------------------\n118: \n119: During ExecInitExpr() and similar routines, Expr trees are converted\n120: into the flat representation.  Each Expr node might be represented by\n121: zero, one, or more ExprEvalSteps.\n122: \n123: Each ExprEvalStep's work is determined by its opcode (of enum ExprEvalOp)\n124: and it stores the result of its work into the Datum variable and boolean\n125: null flag variable pointed to by ExprEvalStep->resvalue/resnull.\n126: Complex expressions are performed by chaining together several steps.\n127: For example, \"a + b\" (one OpExpr, with two Var expressions) would be\n128: represented as two steps to fetch the Var values, and one step for the\n129: evaluation of the function underlying the + operator.  The steps for the\n130: Vars would have their resvalue/resnull pointing directly to the appropriate\n131: args[].value .isnull elements in the FunctionCallInfoBaseData struct that\n132: is used by the function evaluation step, thus avoiding extra work to copy\n133: the result values around.\n134: \n135: The last entry in a completed ExprState->steps array is always an\n136: EEOP_DONE_RETURN or EEOP_DONE_NO_RETURN step; this removes the need to\n137: test for end-of-array while iterating. The former is used when the\n138: expression returns a value directly, the latter when side-effects of\n139: expression initialization are the goal (e.g. for projection or\n140: aggregate transition value computation).\n141: \n142: Also, if the expression contains any variable references (to user\n143: columns of the ExprContext's INNER, OUTER, or SCAN tuples), the steps\n144: array begins with EEOP_*_FETCHSOME steps that ensure that the relevant\n145: tuples have been deconstructed to make the required columns directly\n146: available (cf. slot_getsomeattrs()).  This allows individual Var-fetching\n147: steps to be little more than an array lookup.\n148: \n149: Most of ExecInitExpr()'s work is done by the recursive function\n150: ExecInitExprRec() and its subroutines.  ExecInitExprRec() maps one Expr\n151: ",
    "url": "https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/postgres/postgres@0eeffd31bf9bb73446387cd7eebbbf43148aa281/-/blob/src/backend/executor/README%3FL1-150"
  }

The grammar (bison, gram.y) reduces these tokens using shift-reduce parsing to produce an abstract syntax tree. The raw_parser() function in parser.c orchestrates this by initializing the flex scanner and bison parser, returning a list of RawStmt nodes. RawStmt is a simple wrapper containing the statement node (SelectStmt, InsertStmt, etc.) and location information.\\n\\n**Analysis & Rewriting Stage (analyze.c + rewrite/):**\\nThe semantic analyzer (parse_analyze_fixedparams in analyze.c) transforms each RawStmt into a Query node through multiple passes:\\n- Name resolution: tables, columns, functions\\n- Type inference and coercion\\n- Predicate pushing and qualification processing\\n- Aggregate and window function detection\\nThis produces a normalized Query structure. The rewriter (QueryRewrite in rewriteHandler.c) then applies rules - transforming views into their base query and applying INSTEAD rules for triggers. Output is a list of Query nodes, potentially expanded from a single input.\\n\\n**Two-Phase Optimization (planner.c, allpaths.c, createplan.c):**\\nThe optimizer implements a classic two-phase approach:\\nPhase 1 (Path Generation - allpaths.c):\\n- For each base table, enumerate all applicable access methods (sequential scan, index scans, bitmap scans) creating Paths\\n- For each table, precompute selectivity estimates and potential parameterizations\\n- Build a RelOptInfo structure for each base table and each possible join combination\\n- Use dynamic programming to systematically consider all join orderings\\n- For each join pair, generate Paths for each join method (nested loop, merge join, hash join)\\n- Maintain a Pareto-optimal set of Paths based on cost estimates (different Paths may be optimal for different output orderings or row counts)\\n\\nPhase 2 (Plan Creation - createplan.c):\\n- Select the cheapest Path for the final RelOptInfo (covering all relations)\\n- Call create_plan() recursively to convert the Path tree into a Plan tree\\n- This process selects specific operators and fills in execution parameters\\n- The result is a Plan tree that mirrors the Path tree structure\\n- Wrap the Plan in a PlannedStmt which contains metadata for execution (parameter types, resource relations, etc.)\\n\\n**Executor Stage (execProcnode.c, execMain.c):**\\nThe executor implements a Volcano-style pull-based pipeline:\\nInitialization (ExecInitNode):\\n- ExecInitNode() dispatches on Plan node type (via switch statement)\\n- For each Plan node type (SeqScan, NestLoop, Agg, etc.), calls the corresponding ExecInit* function\\n- This creates a parallel tree of PlanState nodes that mirror the Plan tree\\n- PlanState nodes hold runtime state while Plan nodes remain read-only\\n- Each node is initialized recursively, building the entire state tree bottom-up\\n\\nExecution (ExecProcNode):\\n- ExecProcNode() is called repeatedly on the root PlanState\\n- This macro/function uses either function pointers or a dispatch table\\n- Each node type implements an ExecXxx function (ExecSeqScan, ExecNestLoop, etc.)\\n- Each function produces the next tuple by calling ExecProcNode on child nodes\\n- Child nodes are called in a demand-pull model - parents request tuples as needed\\n- When a child is exhausted (returns NULL), the parent adjusts its iteration\\n- Tuples flow up the tree as TupleTableSlot pointers\\n\\n**Data Structure Transformations:**\\nSQL String \u2192 RawStmt (parsing) \u2192 Query (analysis/rewrite) \u2192 Path nodes & RelOptInfo (optimization phase 1) \u2192 Plan nodes in PlannedStmt (optimization phase 2) \u2192 PlanState tree mirroring Plan tree (execution initialization) \u2192 Tuples via ExecProcNode dispatch (execution).\\n\\n**Design Patterns Identified:**\\n1. Volcano Architecture: Pull-based pipeline where parent nodes request tuples from children\\n2. Type-based Dispatch: Switch statements and function pointers for handling node types\\n3. Immutable Intermediate Structures: RawStmt/Query/Plan are transformed, not modified\\n4. State Mirror Pattern: PlanState mirrors Plan tree structure but holds mutable execution state\\n5. Two-Phase Optimization: Separate path enumeration and plan selection phases\\n6. Cost-Based Optimization: All Paths are ranked by estimated cost; cheapest is chosen\\n7. Recursive Tree Processing: Each stage processes the tree recursively (parsing, analysis, planning, execution initialization)\"\n  }\n}\n",

\u2502     \u2502  \u2502  \u251c\u2500 indxpath.c: generate_index_paths()\n  \u2502              \u2502     \u2502  \u2502  \u2514\u2500 costsize.c: estimate_rel_size()\n  \u2502              \u2502     \u2502  \u2502\n  \u2502              \u2502     \u2502  \u2514\u2500 Dynamic programming join ordering:\n  \u2502              \u2502     \u2502     \u251c\u2500 For join size 2: make_rels_by_clause_joins()\n  \u2502              \u2502     \u2502     \u251c\u2500 generate_join_permutations()\n  \u2502              \u2502     \u2502     \u251c\u2500 For each join pair:\n  \u2502              \u2502     \u2502     \u2502  \u251c\u2500 Nested loop path: create_nestloop_path()\n  \u2502              \u2502     \u2502     \u2502  \u251c\u2500 Merge join path: create_mergejoin_path()\n  \u2502              \u2502     \u2502     \u2502  \u2514\u2500 Hash join path: create_hashjoin_path()\n  \u2502              \u2502     \u2502     \u2514\u2500 Select cheapest paths per join rel\n  \u2502              \u2502     \u2502\n  \u2502              \u2502     \u2514\u2500 Output: RelOptInfo with pathlist for each relation\n  \u2502              \u2502\n  \u2502              \u251c\u2500 fetch_upper_rel(UPPERREL_FINAL)  [Get final RelOptInfo]\n  \u2502              \u251c\u2500 get_cheapest_fractional_path()   [Select best Path]\n  \u2502              \u2502\n  \u2502              \u2514\u2500 create_plan(best_path)           [PHASE 2: Plan Creation]\n  \u2502                 \u251c\u2500 convert_path_to_plan()\n  \u2502                 \u2502  \u251c\u2500 T_SeqScan \u2192 create_seqscan_plan()\n  \u2502                 \u2502  \u251c\u2500 T_IndexScan \u2192 create_indexscan_plan()\n  \u2502                 \u2502  \u251c\u2500 T_NestLoopPath \u2192 create_nestloop_plan()\n  \u2502                 \u2502  \u2502  \u251c\u2500 create_plan(left_path)\n  \u2502                 \u2502  \u2502  \u251c\u2500 create_plan(right_path)\n  \u2502                 \u2502  \u2502  \u2514\u2500 Make NestLoop Plan node\n  \u2502                 \u2502  \u251c\u2500 T_HashJoinPath \u2192 create_hashjoin_plan()\n  \u2502                 \u2502  \u251c\u2500 T_AggPath \u2192 create_agg_plan()\n  \u2502                 \u2502  \u251c\u2500 T_GroupPath \u2192 create_group_plan()\n  \u2502                 \u2502  \u2514\u2500 ... [other path types]\n  \u2502                 \u2502\n  \u2502                 \u251c\u2500 set_plan_references()  [Fix Var/Param references]\n  \u2502                 \u251c\u2500 final_rel->cheapest_path = NULL\n  \u2502                 \u2502\n  \u2502                 \u2514\u2500 Output: Plan tree structure\n  \u2502\n  \u2502  Output: List<PlannedStmt> (one per Query)\n  \u2502\n  \u251c\u2500 CreatePortal(\"\")                       [Create unnamed portal]\n  \u251c\u2500 PortalDefineQuery(portal, plantree_list)\n  \u2502\n  \u251c\u2500 PortalStart(portal)                    [EXECUTE STAGE - Initialization]\n  \u2502  \u2514\u2500 ExecutorStart()\n  \u2502     \u2514\u2500 ExecInitNode(plan_tree_root)\n  \u2502        \u251c\u2500 Switch on node type (nodeTag):\n  \u2502        \u251c\u2500 T_SeqScan \u2192 ExecInitSeqScan()\n  \u2502        \u2502  \u251c\u2500 Create SeqScanState with plan pointer\n  \u2502        \u2502  \u251c\u2500 Initialize child plans (recursive)\n  \u2502        \u2502  \u2514\u2500 Store reference to opened table\n  \u2502        \u251c\u2500 T_NestLoop \u2192 ExecInitNestLoop()\n  \u2502        \u2502  \u251c\u2500 Create NestLoopState\n  \u2502        \u2502  \u251c\u2500 ExecInitNode(outer plan)\n  \u2502        \u2502  \u251c\u2500 ExecInitNode(inner plan)\n  \u2502        \u2502  \u2514\u2500 Initialize join state variables\n  \u2502        \u251c\u2500 T_HashJoin \u2192 ExecInitHashJoin()\n  \u2502        \u251c\u2500 T_Agg \u2192 ExecInitAgg()\n  \u2502        \u2514\u2500 ... [recursive for all nodes]\n  \u2502\n  \u2502  Output: PlanState tree (mirrors Plan tree structure)\n  \u2502\n  \u2514\u2500 PortalRun(portal, FETCH_ALL, receiver)  [EXECUTE STAGE - Tuple Pulling]\n     \u2514\u2500 ExecutorRun()\n        \u2514\u2500 ExecutePlan()\n           \u2514\u2500 while (!done):\n              \u2514\u2500 TupleTableSlot *slot = ExecProcNode(root_planstate)\n                 \u2502\n                 \u251c\u2500 ExecProcNode dispatches to node type:\n                 \u251c\u2500 ExecSeqScan(planstate)\n                 \u2502  \u251c\u2500 heap_getnext() from table\n                 \u2502  \u251c\u2500 ExecFilterExpr(qual, tuple)\n                 \u2502  \u2514\u2500 Return TupleTableSlot\n                 \u2502\n                 \u251c\u2500 ExecNestLoop(planstate)\n                 \u2502  \u251c\u2500 outerTuple = ExecProcNode(outer_plan)\n                 \u2502  \u251c\u2500 innerTuple = ExecProcNode(inner_plan)\n                 \u2502  \u251c\u2500 Check join condition\n                 \u2502  \u2514\u2500 Return joined TupleTableSlot\n                 \u2502\n                 \u251c\u2500 ExecHashJoin(planstate)\n                 \u2502  \u251c\u2500 If first call: build hash table from inner\n                 \u2502  \u251c\u2500 For each outer tuple: probe hash table\n                 \u2502  \u2514\u2500 Return matching tuples\n                 \u2502\n                 \u251c\u2500 ExecAgg(planstate)\n                 \u2502  \u251c\u2500 Accumulate aggregates from input\n                 \u2502  \u251c\u2500 ExecProcNode(input_plan)\n                 \u2502  \u2514\u2500 Return aggregated results\n                 \u2502\n                 \u2514\u2500 ... [continue until exhausted]\n\n        Output: Tuple stream to client via receiver\n```\n\n## Analysis\n\n### Design Patterns Identified\n\n#### 1. **Volcano Architecture (Iterator Model)**\nPostgreSQL implements the classic Volcano execution model with a pull-based pipeline:\n- **Data Flow**: Upward from leaves (scan nodes) to root\n- **Control Flow**: Each parent calls child's ExecProcNode() to request tuples\n- **Backpressure**: Child waits for parent to request before producing\n- **Tuples**: Transferred as TupleTableSlot pointers through the tree\n\nThis is a **demand-pull model**, contrasted with push-based execution where operators push results to consumers.\n\n#### 2. **Type-Based Dispatch via Function Pointers and Switch Statements**\nTwo dispatch mechanisms are used:\n- **Initialization**: ExecInitNode() uses a giant switch statement on nodeTag\n- **Execution**: ExecProcNode() either calls ps->ExecProcNode function pointer or dispatches via table lookup\n\nThis avoids virtual functions in C while maintaining flexibility for custom nodes.\n\n#### 3. **State Mirror Pattern**\nThe executor maintains two parallel tree structures:\n- **Plan Tree**: Immutable, read-only description of execution strategy\n  - Produced by optimizer\n  - Shared across multiple executions (plan caching)\n  - Contains only planning-time information\n- **PlanState Tree**: Mutable, runtime state\n  - Created at execution start\n  - Destroyed at execution end\n  - Contains cursors, accumulated values, buffers, etc.\n  - Each PlanState has a `Plan *plan` pointer to its corresponding Plan node\n\nThis separation enables **plan caching and reuse** without side effects.\n\n#### 4. **Two-Phase Optimization**\nThe optimizer explicitly separates path enumeration from plan selection:\n\n**Phase 1: Path Generation (allpaths.c)**\n- Enumerate ALL candidate execution paths\n- Each Path represents one way to access/join data\n- Multiple Paths coexist for the same relation\n- Cost estimates guide selection but all are generated\n- Example: Table can be accessed via sequential scan, index scan 1, index scan 2, bitmap scan\n- Paths parameterized by sort order and filtering level\n\n**Phase 2: Plan Creation (createplan.c)**\n- Select the single cheapest Path (or tied cheapest)\n- Convert Path tree to Plan tree\n- Plan contains the selected operators and final parameters\n- One Plan per Query (unlike multiple Paths)\n\nThis separation allows:\n- Costs to be estimated without committing to a plan\n- Plan caching since multiple paths aren't carried forward\n- Analysis of alternative paths (for EXPLAIN)\n\n#### 5. **Immutable Intermediate Structures (Functional Style)**\nEach pipeline stage produces new structures without modifying inputs:\n- `RawStmt` \u2192 `Query` (analyzer doesn't modify RawStmt)\n- `Query` \u2192 `Query` (rewriter produces new Query list)\n- `Query` \u2192 Path/RelOptInfo (planner builds new structures)\n- `Path` \u2192 `Plan` (createplan builds Plan without modifying Path)\n\nThis supports:\n- Error recovery (transaction abort reverts to consistent state)\n- Plan caching (original query trees preserved)\n- Multiple rewrite possibilities for one query\n\n#### 6. **Cost-Based Optimization with Dynamic Programming**\nThe join order optimizer uses DP to avoid exponential blowup:\n- For N tables: builds all 2-table joins, then 3-table, etc.\n- At each level, keeps only Pareto-optimal paths per join relation\n- A join RelOptInfo may have 5-10 Paths (different methods/orders)\n- Final result: single best Path for all-table join\n\nWithout DP: exploring all join orders is O(2^N)\nWith DP: exploring with pruning is O(N^3) to O(N^4)\n\n#### 7. **Recursive Tree Processing**\nAll stages process trees recursively:\n- **Parser**: grammar recursion produces nested parse trees\n- **Analyzer**: transforms nested statement trees recursively\n- **Planner**: recursive subquery planning for subselects\n- **Executor**: ExecInitNode recursively initializes children\n\n#### 8. **Expression Flattening for Efficient Evaluation**\nExpression trees from parser/analyzer are compiled to flat arrays:\n- **Parse Tree**: Tree structure with Expr nodes\n- **ExprState**: Flat array of ExprEvalStep structs evaluated sequentially\n- **Benefits**:\n  - Single function call loop (interpreter)\n  - Easy to JIT compile to native code\n  - Reduced memory fragmentation\n  - Cache-friendly sequential access\n\n### Component Responsibilities\n\n#### Traffic Cop (tcop/postgres.c)\n- Receives query string from network protocol\n- Coordinates calls to each pipeline stage\n- Handles transactions (BEGIN/COMMIT/ABORT)\n- Manages query logging and statistics\n- Returns results to client via DestReceiver\n\n#### Parser (parser/)\n- Converts text SQL to abstract syntax\n- Produces type-tagged node structures (RawStmt)\n- Does NOT perform semantic checking\n- Rejects syntactically invalid SQL\n\n#### Analyzer (parser/analyze.c + parse_*.c)\n- Validates semantic correctness\n- Resolves table/column names to OIDs\n- Infers and coerces data types\n- Extracts predicates and qualifications\n- Produces normalized Query trees\n\n#### Rewriter (rewrite/)\n- Applies View expansion (replace view with its definition)\n- Executes INSTEAD OF rules\n- Handles trigger-like query transformations\n- May expand 1 Query into many\n\n#### Optimizer (optimizer/)\n- Enumerates all viable execution strategies (Paths)\n- Estimates costs based on table statistics\n- Selects cheapest strategy (or tied for cheapest)\n- Produces final Plan tree\n\n#### Executor (executor/)\n- Initializes runtime state from Plan tree\n- Pulls tuples from leaf nodes upward\n- Applies predicates, projections, aggregations\n- Returns results to client\n\n### Data Flow and Type Transformations\n\n```\nText String\n    \u2193 [scan.l: tokenize]\nToken Stream\n    \u2193 [gram.y: parse]\nRawStmt (unanalyzed parse tree)\n    \u251c\u2500 SelectStmt, InsertStmt, DeleteStmt, UpdateStmt, etc.\n    \u2514\u2500 stmt_location, stmt_len\n\n    \u2193 [analyze.c: semantic analysis]\nQuery (normalized, semantic information extracted)\n    \u251c\u2500 commandType: CMD_SELECT, CMD_INSERT, etc.\n    \u251c\u2500 targetList: selected/returned columns\n    \u251c\u2500 fromClause: (processed into rtable)\n    \u251c\u2500 whereClause: predicates\n    \u251c\u2500 rtable: RangeTblEntry list (table references)\n    \u251c\u2500 rteperminfos: permission checks\n    \u2514\u2500 Various specialized fields for each command\n\n    \u2193 [rewrite.c: rule application]\nList<Query> (potentially expanded)\n\n    \u2193 [planner.c: phase 1 path generation]\nRelOptInfo + Path structures (cost estimation)\n    \u251c\u2500 RelOptInfo per base relation and each join combination\n    \u2502  \u251c\u2500 pathlist: List<Path> all viable access methods\n    \u2502  \u251c\u2500 cheapest_startup_path: fastest first row\n    \u2502  \u251c\u2500 cheapest_total_path: fastest all rows\n    \u2502  \u2514\u2500 rows, width estimates\n    \u2514\u2500 Path subtypes:\n       \u251c\u2500 SeqScanPath, IndexPath, BitmapHeapPath\n       \u251c\u2500 NestLoopPath, MergeJoinPath, HashJoinPath\n       \u251c\u2500 AppendPath, GroupPath, AggPath, etc.\n       \u2514\u2500 startup_cost, total_cost, rows, pathkeys\n\n    \u2193 [createplan.c: phase 2 plan creation]\nPlannedStmt (final executable plan)\n    \u251c\u2500 magic: validation magic number\n    \u251c\u2500 commandType: CMD_SELECT, etc.\n    \u251c\u2500 planTree: Plan node tree root\n    \u251c\u2500 rtable: RangeTblEntry list for plan\n    \u251c\u2500 resultRelations: relations being modified\n    \u251c\u2500 paramExecTypes: parameter data types\n    \u2514\u2500 Metadata for cache invalidation, permissions, etc.\n\n    \u2514\u2500 planTree structure (Plan nodes):\n       \u251c\u2500 SeqScan, IndexScan, BitmapHeapScan (leaves)\n       \u251c\u2500 NestLoop, MergeJoin, HashJoin (join operators)\n       \u251c\u2500 Agg, Sort, Limit, Unique, Append (unary)\n       \u251c\u2500 Each Plan has:\n       \u2502  \u251c\u2500 targetlist: columns to output\n       \u2502  \u251c\u2500 qual: filter predicates\n       \u2502  \u251c\u2500 plan_node_id: unique identifier\n       \u2502  \u251c\u2500 startup_cost, total_cost: from optimizer\n       \u2502  \u2514\u2500 [operator-specific fields]\n       \u2502\n       \u2514\u2500 Children: left_plan, right_plan (for binary ops)\n\n    \u2193 [execProcnode.c: ExecInitNode]\nPlanState tree (executor state mirroring Plan tree)\n    \u251c\u2500 Each PlanState corresponds to Plan node\n    \u251c\u2500 Plan *plan pointer to const Plan\n    \u251c\u2500 State-specific fields:\n    \u2502  \u251c\u2500 Scan nodes: table cursor, buffer\n    \u2502  \u251c\u2500 Join nodes: tuples being joined, hash tables\n    \u2502  \u251c\u2500 Agg nodes: state for accumulating aggregates\n    \u2502  \u2514\u2500 etc.\n    \u2514\u2500 Child PlanState pointers\n\n    \u2193 [ExecutorRun \u2192 ExecProcNode loop]\nTupleTableSlot stream (tuples to client)\n    \u251c\u2500 One TupleTableSlot at a time\n    \u251c\u2500 NULL slot signals end of data\n    \u2514\u2500 Each slot contains:\n       \u251c\u2500 column values (Datum array)\n       \u251c\u2500 NULL flags (bool array)\n       \u2514\u2500 tuple descriptor\n```\n\n### Volcano-Style Executor Dispatch (execProcnode.c)\n\nThe ExecProcNode dispatch mechanism implements the Volcano iterator model:\n\n**Initialization Time (ExecInitNode):**\n```c\nPlanState *ExecInitNode(Plan *node, EState *estate, int eflags) {\n    switch (nodeTag(node)) {\n        case T_SeqScan:\n            return ExecInitSeqScan((SeqScan*) node, estate, eflags);\n        case T_NestLoop:\n            return ExecInitNestLoop((NestLoop*) node, estate, eflags);\n        // ... 40+ cases ...\n    }\n}\n```\nEach ExecInitXxx function:\n- Creates a PlanState-derived structure\n- Initializes executor state variables\n- Recursively calls ExecInitNode on children\n- Sets ps->ExecProcNode to function pointer of executor function\n- Returns the new PlanState\n\n**Execution Time (ExecProcNode):**\n```c\nTupleTableSlot *ExecProcNode(PlanState *node) {\n    return (*node->ExecProcNode)(node);  // Function pointer dispatch\n}\n```\n\nAlternative implementations:\n- Jump table (array of function pointers indexed by node type)\n- Direct switch statement (avoids branch prediction stress)\n\n**Why This Design?**\n- **Extensibility**: Custom nodes register their dispatch function\n- **Performance**: Function pointer avoids switch overhead\n- **Polymorphism**: Achieves virtual function behavior in C\n- **Specialization**: First call might be ExecProcNodeFirst() which initializes then switches to faster version\n\n### Two-Phase Optimization in Detail\n\n#### Phase 1: Path Generation (allpaths.c)\n\n```\nQuery with 3 tables: SELECT * FROM a, b, c WHERE a.id=b.id AND b.id=c.id\n\nStep 1: Base relation paths for each table\n  RelOptInfo for table 'a':\n    \u251c\u2500 Path: SeqScan (cost: scan cost)\n    \u251c\u2500 Path: IndexScan on a.id (cost: index lookup)\n    \u2514\u2500 Path: BitmapHeapScan using index (cost: bitmap + heap scan)\n\n  RelOptInfo for table 'b': [similar paths]\n  RelOptInfo for table 'c': [similar paths]\n\nStep 2: Join 2 tables using DP (join size = 2)\n  Available joins: {a,b}, {b,c}, {a,c}\n\n  For {a,b}:\n    Join clause: a.id = b.id\n\n    RelOptInfo for {a,b}:\n      \u251c\u2500 Path: NestLoop, outer=a.seqscan, inner=b.idxscan\n      \u2502  (cost: a_cost + a_rows * b_index_cost)\n      \u251c\u2500 Path: HashJoin, outer=a.seqscan, inner=b.seqscan\n      \u2502  (cost: a_cost + b_cost + hash_cost)\n      \u2514\u2500 Path: MergeJoin, outer=a.sorted, inner=b.sorted\n         (cost: a_sort_cost + b_sort_cost + merge_cost)\n\n    Keep 2-3 cheapest paths\n\nStep 3: Join 3 tables (join size = 3)\n  Available joins: {a,b,c} using ({a,b}, c) or ({b,c}, a)\n\n  For {a,b} \u2192 {a,b,c}:\n    Join clause: b.id = c.id\n    Try NestLoop, HashJoin, MergeJoin with {a,b} and c\n\n  RelOptInfo for {a,b,c}:\n    \u251c\u2500 Path: NestLoop, outer={a,b}.hashjoin, inner=c.seqscan\n    \u251c\u2500 Path: HashJoin, outer={a,b}.mergjoin, inner=c.seqscan\n    \u2514\u2500 etc.\n\n  Keep cheapest path(s)\n\nFinal: Best Path for {a,b,c} selected\n```\n\n#### Phase 2: Plan Creation (createplan.c)\n\n```\nGiven best Path for {a,b,c}:\n  NestLoop(HashJoin(SeqScan(a), SeqScan(b)), SeqScan(c))\n\nconvert_path_to_plan recurses:\n\nNestLoop Path\n  \u2192 create_nestloop_plan()\n    \u251c\u2500 convert_path_to_plan(outer=HashJoin Path)\n    \u2502   \u2192 create_hashjoin_plan()\n    \u2502     \u251c\u2500 convert_path_to_plan(outer=SeqScan(a) Path)\n    \u2502     \u2502   \u2192 create_seqscan_plan()\n    \u2502     \u2502       Create SeqScan Plan node for table 'a'\n    \u2502     \u251c\u2500 convert_path_to_plan(inner=SeqScan(b) Path)\n    \u2502     \u2502   \u2192 create_seqscan_plan()\n    \u2502     \u2502       Create SeqScan Plan node for table 'b'\n    \u2502     \u2514\u2500 Create HashJoin Plan with children\n    \u2502\n    \u251c\u2500 convert_path_to_plan(inner=SeqScan(c) Path)\n    \u2502   \u2192 create_seqscan_plan()\n    \u2502       Create SeqScan Plan node for table 'c'\n    \u2502\n    \u2514\u2500 Create NestLoop Plan with children\n\nFinal PlannedStmt with Plan tree:\n  PlannedStmt {\n    planTree: NestLoop {\n      left: HashJoin {\n        left: SeqScan(a)\n        right: SeqScan(b)\n      }\n      right: SeqScan(c)\n    }\n  }\n```\n\n## Summary\n\nPostgreSQL's query execution pipeline exemplifies classic database architecture patterns. The **traffic cop** (`exec_simple_query`) orchestrates a **staged pipeline** from text SQL to tuples: **parsing** (lexer+grammar) transforms text to RawStmt, **analysis** (semantic checking) produces Query nodes, **rewriting** (rule application) expands queries, **optimization** (two-phase: paths then plans) selects the best execution strategy, and **execution** (Volcano iterator model) implements the plan via demand-pull tuple flow.\n\nThe design separates concerns clearly: the **immutable, read-only Plan tree** produced by optimization is never modified during execution, while a parallel **PlanState tree** holds mutable runtime state. This enables plan caching, reuse, and clean transaction semantics.\n\nThe **two-phase optimizer** explores all viable Paths through dynamic programming before committing to a single Plan, balancing exhaustive search against exponential blowup. The **Volcano executor** uses function pointers or jump tables to dispatch tuple-pulling calls to node-specific implementations, achieving polymorphism without virtual functions in C.\n\nThroughout, **recursive tree processing** and **type-based dispatch** via tagged node structures provide flexibility while maintaining C's performance characteristics. This 30+-year-old architecture remains the industry standard for relational databases.\n",

**src/include/nodes/plannodes.h** \u2014 Plan and PlannedStmt node types\n- **src/include/nodes/primnodes.h** \u2014 Expression primitive types (Var, Const, OpExpr, etc.)\n- **src/include/nodes/execnodes.h** \u2014 Executor state node types (PlanState, ScanState, JoinState, etc.)\n- **src/include/nodes/pathnodes.h** \u2014 Path and RelOptInfo structures for optimization\n\n### Documentation\n- **src/backend/optimizer/README** \u2014 Optimizer architecture and concepts\n- **src/backend/executor/README** \u2014 Executor architecture and concepts\n- **src/backend/parser/README** \u2014 Parser architecture\n\n\n## Dependency Chain\n\n### Entry Point: exec_simple_query()\n**File**: src/backend/tcop/postgres.c, line 1016\n\n```\nexec_simple_query(const char *query_string)\n  \u251c\u2500 start_xact_command()                    [transaction setup]\n  \u2502\n  \u251c\u2500 pg_parse_query(query_string)       \u251c\u2500 subquery_planner()           [PHASE 1: Path Generation]\n  \u2502              \u2502  \u2514\u2500 query_planner()           [Main join planning]\n  \u2502              \u2502 \u2502     \u2502  \u2502  \u251c\u2500 indxpath.c: generate_index_paths()\n  \u2502              \u2502     \u2502  \u2502  \u2514\u2500 costsize.c: estimate_rel_size()\n  \u2502              \u2502     \u2502  \u2502\n  \u2502              \u2502     \u2502  \u2514\u2500 Dynamic programming join ordering:\n  \u2502              \u2502     \u2502     \u251c\u2500 For join size 2: [recursive for all nodes]\n  \u2502\n  \u2502  Output: PlanState tree (mirrors Plan tree structure)\n  \u2502\n  \u2514\u2500 PortalRun(portal, FETCH_ALL, receiver)  [EXECUTE STAGE - Tuple Pulling]\n     \u2514\u2500 ExecutorRun()\n        \u2514\u2500 ExecutePlan()\n           \u2514\u2500 while (!done):\n              \u2514\u2500 TupleTableSlot *slot = ExecProcNode(root_planstate)\n                 \u2502\n                 \u251c\u2500 ExecProcNode dispatches to node type:\n                 \u251c\u2500 ExecSeqScan(planstate)\n                 \u2502  \u251c\u2500 heap_getnext() from table\n                 \u2502  \u251c\u2500 ExecFilterExpr(qual, tuple)\n                 \u2502  \u2514\u2500 Return TupleTableSlot\n                 \u2502\n                 \u251c\u2500 ExecNestLoop(planstate)\n                 \u2502  \u251c\u2500 outerTuple = ExecProcNode(outer_plan)\n                 \u2502  \u251c\u2500 innerTuple = ExecProcNode(inner_plan)\n                 \u2502  \u251c\u2500 Check join condition\n                 \u2502  \u2514\u2500 Return joined TupleTableSlot\n                 \u2502\n                 \u251c\u2500 ExecHashJoin(planstate)\n                 \u2502  \u251c\u2500 If first call: build hash table from inner\n                 \u2502  \u251c\u2500 For each outer tuple: probe hash table\n                 \u2502  \u2514\u2500 Return matching tuples\n                 \u2502\n                 \u251c\u2500 ExecAgg(planstate)\n                 \u2502  \u251c\u2500 Accumulate aggregates from input\n                 \u2502  \u251c\u2500 ExecProcNode(input_plan)\n                 \u2502  \u2514\u2500 Return aggregated results\n                 \u2502\n                 \u2514\u2500 ... [continue until exhausted]\n\n        Output: Tuple stream to client via receiver\n```\n\n## Analysis\n\n### Design Patterns Identified\n\n#### 1. **Volcano Architecture (Iterator Model)**\nPostgreSQL implements the classic Volcano execution model with a pull-based pipeline:\n- **Data Flow**: Upward from leaves (scan nodes) to root\n- **Control Flow**: Each parent calls child's ExecProcNode() to request tuples\n- **Backpressure**: Child waits for parent to request before producing\n- **Tuples**: Transferred as TupleTableSlot pointers through the tree\n\nThis is a **demand-pull model**, contrasted with push-based execution where operators push results to consumers.\n\n#### 2. **Type-Based Dispatch via Function Pointers and Switch Statements**\nTwo dispatch mechanisms are used:\n- **Initialization**: ExecInitNode() uses a giant switch statement on nodeTag\n- **Execution**: ExecProcNode() either calls ps->ExecProcNode function pointer or dispatches via table lookup\n\nThis avoids virtual functions in C while maintaining flexibility for custom nodes.\n\n#### 3. **State Mirror Pattern**\nThe executor maintains two parallel tree structures:\n- **Plan Tree**: Immutable, read-only description of execution strategy\n  - Produced by optimizer\n  - Shared across multiple executions (plan caching)\n  - Contains only planning-time information\n- **PlanState Tree**: Mutable, runtime state\n  - Created at execution start\n  - Destroyed at execution end\n  - Contains cursors, accumulated values, buffers, etc.\n  - Each PlanState has a `Plan *plan` pointer to its corresponding Plan node\n\nThis separation enables **plan caching and reuse** without side effects.\n\n#### 4. **Two-Phase Optimization**\nThe optimizer explicitly separates path enumeration from plan selection:\n\n**Phase 1: Path Generation (allpaths.c)**\n- Enumerate ALL candidate execution paths\n- Each Path represents one way to access/join data\n- Multiple Paths coexist for the same relation\n- Cost estimates guide selection but all are generated\n- Example: Table can be accessed via sequential scan, index scan 1, index scan 2, bitmap scan\n- Paths parameterized by sort order and filtering level\n\n**Phase 2: Plan Creation (createplan.c)**\n- Select the single cheapest Path (or tied cheapest)\n- Convert Path tree to Plan tree\n- Plan contains the selected operators and final parameters\n- One Plan per Query (unlike multiple Paths)\n\nThis separation allows:\n- Costs to be estimated without committing to a plan\n- Plan caching since multiple paths aren't carried forward\n- Analysis of alternative paths (for EXPLAIN)\n\n#### 5. **Immutable Intermediate Structures (Functional Style)**\nEach pipeline stage produces new structures without modifying inputs:\n- `RawStmt` \u2192 `Query` (analyzer doesn't modify RawStmt)\n- `Query` \u2192 `Query` (rewriter produces new Query list)\n- `Query` \u2192 Path/RelOptInfo (planner builds new structures)\n- `Path` \u2192 `Plan` (createplan builds Plan without modifying Path)\n\nThis supports:\n- Error recovery (transaction abort reverts to consistent state)\n- Plan caching (original query trees preserved)\n- Multiple rewrite possibilities for one query\n\n#### 6. **Cost-Based Optimization with Dynamic Programming**\nThe join order optimizer uses DP to avoid exponential blowup:\n- For N tables: builds all 2-table joins, then 3-table, etc.\n- At each level, keeps only Pareto-optimal paths per join relation\n- A join RelOptInfo may have 5-10 Paths (different methods/orders)\n- Final result: single best Path for all-table join\n\nWithout DP: exploring all join orders is O(2^N)\nWith DP: exploring with pruning is O(N^3) to O(N^4)\n\n#### 7. **Recursive Tree Processing**\nAll stages process trees recursively:\n- **Parser**: grammar recursion produces nested parse trees\n- **Analyzer**: transforms nested statement trees recursively\n- **Planner**: recursive subquery planning for subselects\n- **Executor**: ExecInitNode recursively initializes children\n\n#### 8. **Expression Flattening for Efficient Evaluation**\nExpression trees from parser/analyzer are compiled to flat arrays:\n- **Parse Tree**: Tree structure with Expr nodes\n- **ExprState**: Flat array of ExprEvalStep structs evaluated sequentially\n- **Benefits**:\n  - Single function call loop (interpreter)\n  - Easy to JIT compile to native code\n  - Reduced memory fragmentation\n  - Cache-friendly sequential access\n\n### Component Responsibilities\n\n#### Traffic Cop (tcop/postgres.c)\n- Receives query string from network protocol\n- Coordinates calls to each pipeline stage\n- Handles transactions (BEGIN/COMMIT/ABORT)\n- Manages query logging and statistics\n- Returns results to client via DestReceiver\n\n#### Parser (parser/)\n- Converts text SQL to abstract syntax\n- Produces type-tagged node structures (RawStmt)\n- Does NOT perform semantic checking\n- Rejects syntactically invalid SQL\n\n#### Analyzer (parser/analyze.c + parse_*.c)\n- Validates semantic correctness\n- Resolves table/column names to OIDs\n- Infers and coerces data types\n- Extracts predicates and qualifications\n- Produces normalized Query trees\n\n#### Rewriter (rewrite/)\n- Applies View expansion (replace view with its definition)\n- Executes INSTEAD OF rules\n- Handles trigger-like query transformations\n- May expand 1 Query into many\n\n#### Optimizer (optimizer/)\n- Enumerates all viable execution strategies (Paths)\n- Estimates costs based on table statistics\n- Selects cheapest strategy (or tied for cheapest)\n- Produces final Plan tree\n\n#### Executor (executor/)\n- Initializes runtime state from Plan tree\n- Pulls tuples from leaf nodes upward\n- Applies predicates, projections, aggregations\n- Returns results to client\n\n### Data Flow and Type Transformations\n\n```\nText String\n    \u2193 [scan.l: tokenize]\nToken Stream\n    \u2193 [gram.y: parse]\nRawStmt (unanalyzed parse tree)\n    \u251c\u2500 SelectStmt, InsertStmt, DeleteStmt, UpdateStmt, etc.\n    \u2514\u2500 stmt_location, stmt_len\n\n    \u2193 [analyze.c: semantic analysis]\nQuery (normalized, semantic information extracted)\n    \u251c\u2500 commandType: CMD_SELECT, CMD_INSERT, etc.\n    \u251c\u2500 targetList: selected/returned columns\n    \u251c\u2500 fromClause: (processed into rtable)\n    \u251c\u2500 whereClause: predicates\n    \u251c\u2500 rtable: RangeTblEntry list (table references)\n    \u251c\u2500 rteperminfos: permission checks\n    \u2514\u2500 Various specialized fields for each command\n\n    \u2193 [rewrite.c: rule application]\nList<Query> (potentially expanded)\n\n    \u2193 [planner.c: phase 1 path generation]\nRelOptInfo + Path structures (cost estimation)\n    \u251c\u2500 RelOptInfo per base relation and each join combination\n    \u2502  \u251c\u2500 pathlist: List<Path> all viable access methods\n    \u2502  \u251c\u2500 cheapest_startup_path: fastest first row\n    \u2502  \u251c\u2500 cheapest_total_path: fastest all rows\n    \u2502  \u2514\u2500 rows, width estimates\n    \u2514\u2500 Path subtypes:\n       \u251c\u2500 SeqScanPath, IndexPath, BitmapHeapPath\n       \u251c\u2500 NestLoopPath, MergeJoinPath, HashJoinPath\n       \u251c\u2500 AppendPath, GroupPath, AggPath, etc.\n       \u2514\u2500 startup_cost, total_cost, rows, pathkeys\n\n    \u2193 [createplan.c: phase 2 plan creation]\nPlannedStmt (final executable plan)\n    \u251c\u2500 magic: validation magic number\n    \u251c\u2500 commandType: CMD_SELECT, etc.\n    \u251c\u2500 planTree: Plan node tree root\n    \u251c\u2500 rtable: RangeTblEntry list for plan\n    \u251c\u2500 resultRelations: relations being modified\n    \u251c\u2500 paramExecTypes: parameter data types\n    \u2514\u2500 Metadata for cache invalidation, permissions, etc.\n\n    \u2514\u2500 planTree structure (Plan nodes):\n       \u251c\u2500 SeqScan, IndexScan, BitmapHeapScan (leaves)\n       \u251c\u2500 NestLoop, MergeJoin, HashJoin (join operators)\n       \u251c\u2500 Agg, Sort, Limit, Unique, Append (unary)\n       \u251c\u2500 Each Plan has:\n       \u2502  \u251c\u2500 targetlist: columns to output\n       \u2502  \u251c\u2500 qual: filter predicates\n       \u2502  \u251c\u2500 plan_node_id: unique identifier\n       \u2502  \u251c\u2500 startup_cost, total_cost: from optimizer\n       \u2502  \u2514\u2500 [operator-specific fields]\n       \u2502\n       \u2514\u2500 Children: left_plan, right_plan (for binary ops)\n\n    \u2193 [execProcnode.c: ExecInitNode]\nPlanState tree (executor state mirroring Plan tree)\n    \u251c\u2500 Each PlanState corresponds to Plan node\n    \u251c\u2500 Plan *plan pointer to const Plan\n    \u251c\u2500 State-specific fields:\n    \u2502  \u251c\u2500 Scan nodes: table cursor, buffer\n    \u2502  \u251c\u2500 Join nodes: tuples being joined, hash tables\n    \u2502  \u251c\u2500 Agg nodes: state for accumulating aggregates\n    \u2502  \u2514\u2500 etc.\n    \u2514\u2500 Child PlanState pointers\n\n    \u2193 [ExecutorRun \u2192 ExecProcNode loop]\nTupleTableSlot stream (tuples to client)\n    \u251c\u2500 One TupleTableSlot at a time\n    \u251c\u2500 NULL slot signals end of data\n    \u2514\u2500 Each slot contains:\n       \u251c\u2500 column values (Datum array)\n       \u251c\u2500 NULL flags (bool array)\n       \u2514\u2500 tuple descriptor\n```\n\n### Volcano-Style Executor Dispatch (execProcnode.c)\n\nThe ExecProcNode dispatch mechanism implements the Volcano iterator model:\n\n**Initialization Time (ExecInitNode):**\n```c\nPlanState *ExecInitNode(Plan *node, EState *estate, int eflags) {\n    switch (nodeTag(node)) {\n        case T_SeqScan:\n            return ExecInitSeqScan((SeqScan*) node, estate, eflags);\n        case T_NestLoop:\n            return ExecInitNestLoop((NestLoop*) node, estate, eflags);\n        // ... 40+ cases ...\n    }\n}\n```\nEach ExecInitXxx function:\n- Creates a PlanState-derived structure\n- Initializes executor state variables\n- Recursively calls ExecInitNode on children\n- Sets ps->ExecProcNode to function pointer of executor function\n- Returns the new PlanState\n\n**Execution Time (ExecProcNode):**\n```c\nTupleTableSlot *ExecProcNode(PlanState *node) {\n    return (*node->ExecProcNode)(node);  // Function pointer dispatch\n}\n```\n\nAlternative implementations:\n- Jump table (array of function pointers indexed by node type)\n- Direct switch statement (avoids branch prediction stress)\n\n**Why This Design?**\n- **Extensibility**: Custom nodes register their dispatch function\n- **Performance**: Function pointer avoids switch overhead\n- **Polymorphism**: Achieves virtual function behavior in C\n- **Specialization**: First call might be ExecProcNodeFirst() which initializes then switches to faster version\n\n### Two-Phase Optimization in Detail\n\n#### Phase 1: Path Generation (allpaths.c)\n\n```\nQuery with 3 tables: SELECT * FROM a, b, c WHERE a.id=b.id AND b.id=c.id\n\nStep 1: Base relation paths for each table\n  RelOptInfo for table 'a':\n    \u251c\u2500 Path: SeqScan (cost: scan cost)\n    \u251c\u2500 Path: IndexScan on a.id (cost: index lookup)\n    \u2514\u2500 Path: BitmapHeapScan using index (cost: bitmap + heap scan)\n\n  RelOptInfo for table 'b': [similar paths]\n  RelOptInfo for table 'c': [similar paths]\n\nStep 2: Join 2 tables using DP (join size = 2)\n  Available joins: {a,b}, {b,c}, {a,c}\n\n  For {a,b}:\n    Join clause: a.id = b.id\n\n    RelOptInfo for {a,b}:\n      \u251c\u2500 Path: NestLoop, outer=a.seqscan, inner=b.idxscan\n      \u2502  (cost: a_cost + a_rows * b_index_cost)\n      \u251c\u2500 Path: HashJoin, outer=a.seqscan, inner=b.seqscan\n      \u2502  (cost: a_cost + b_cost + hash_cost)\n      \u2514\u2500 Path: MergeJoin, outer=a.sorted, inner=b.sorted\n         (cost: a_sort_cost + b_sort_cost + merge_cost)\n\n    Keep 2-3 cheapest paths\n\nStep 3: Join 3 tables (join size = 3)\n  Available joins: {a,b,c} using ({a,b}, c) or ({b,c}, a)\n\n  For {a,b} \u2192 {a,b,c}:\n    Join clause: b.id = c.id\n    Try NestLoop, HashJoin, MergeJoin with {a,b} and c\n\n  RelOptInfo for {a,b,c}:\n    \u251c\u2500 Path: NestLoop, outer={a,b}.hashjoin, inner=c.seqscan\n    \u251c\u2500 Path: HashJoin, outer={a,b}.mergjoin, inner=c.seqscan\n    \u2514\u2500 etc.\n\n  Keep cheapest path(s)\n\nFinal: Best Path for {a,b,c} selected\n```\n\n#### Phase 2: Plan Creation (createplan.c)\n\n```\nGiven best Path for {a,b,c}:\n  NestLoop(HashJoin(SeqScan(a), SeqScan(b)), SeqScan(c))\n\nconvert_path_to_plan recurses:\n\nNestLoop Path\n  \u2192 create_nestloop_plan()\n    \u251c\u2500 convert_path_to_plan(outer=HashJoin Path)\n    \u2502   \u2192 create_hashjoin_plan()\n    \u2502     \u251c\u2500 convert_path_to_plan(outer=SeqScan(a) Path)\n    \u2502     \u2502   \u2192 create_seqscan_plan()\n    \u2502     \u2502       Create SeqScan Plan node for table 'a'\n    \u2502     \u251c\u2500 convert_path_to_plan(inner=SeqScan(b) Path)\n    \u2502     \u2502   \u2192 create_seqscan_plan()\n    \u2502     \u2502       Create SeqScan Plan node for table 'b'\n    \u2502     \u2514\u2500 Create HashJoin Plan with children\n    \u2502\n    \u251c\u2500 convert_path_to_plan(inner=SeqScan(c) Path)\n    \u2502   \u2192 create_seqscan_plan()\n    \u2502       Create SeqScan Plan node for table 'c'\n    \u2502\n    \u2514\u2500 Create NestLoop Plan with children\n\nFinal PlannedStmt with Plan tree:\n  PlannedStmt {\n    planTree: NestLoop {\n      left: HashJoin {\n        left: SeqScan(a)\n        right: SeqScan(b)\n      }\n      right: SeqScan(c)\n    }\n  }\n```\n\n## Summary\n\nPostgreSQL's query execution pipeline exemplifies classic database architecture patterns. The **traffic cop** (`exec_simple_query`) orchestrates a **staged pipeline** from text SQL to tuples: **parsing** (lexer+grammar) transforms text to RawStmt, **analysis** (semantic checking) produces Query nodes, **rewriting** (rule application) expands queries, **optimization** (two-phase: paths then plans) selects the best execution strategy, and **execution** (Volcano iterator model) implements the plan via demand-pull tuple flow.\n\nThe design separates concerns clearly: the **immutable, read-only Plan tree** produced by optimization is never modified during execution, while a parallel **PlanState tree** holds mutable runtime state. This enables plan caching, reuse, and clean transaction semantics.\n\nThe **two-phase optimizer** explores all viable Paths through dynamic programming before committing to a single Plan, balancing exhaustive search against exponential blowup. The **Volcano executor** uses function pointers or jump tables to dispatch tuple-pulling calls to node-specific implementations, achieving polymorphism without virtual functions in C.\n\nThroughout, **recursive tree processing** and **type-based dispatch** via tagged node structures provide flexibility while maintaining C's performance characteristics. This 30+-year-old architecture remains the industry standard for relational databases.\n",

# PostgreSQL Query Execution Pipeline: Complete Architectural Analysis

## Files Examined

### Traffic Cop (Query Orchestration)
- **src/backend/tcop/postgres.c** — Entry point exec_simple_query() orchestrates the entire pipeline, calling parse, analyze, rewrite, plan, and execute stages in sequence

### Parsing Subsystem
- **src/backend/parser/parser.c** — raw_parser() initializes and drives the lexer and parser
- **src/backend/parser/scan.l** — Flex lexical analyzer that tokenizes SQL strings into tokens
- **src/backend/parser/gram.y** — Bison grammar rules that reduce tokens into RawStmt nodes
- **src/backend/parser/analyze.c** — Semantic analyzer (parse_analyze_fixedparams) transforms RawStmt → Query
- **src/backend/parser/parse_*.c** — Specialized analyzers for clauses, expressions, operators, etc.

### Rewriting Subsystem
- **src/backend/rewrite/rewriteHandler.c** — QueryRewrite() applies rules/views transformations
- **src/backend/rewrite/rewriteManip.c** — Utility functions for query rewriting
- **src/backend/rewrite/rowsecurity.c** — Row-level security policy application

### Optimizer Subsystem
#### Phase 1: Path Generation (Enumeration)
- **src/backend/optimizer/path/allpaths.c** — set_base_rel_pathlist() and set_join_pathlist() enumerate all candidate Paths
- **src/backend/optimizer/path/indxpath.c** — Index access path generation
- **src/backend/optimizer/path/joinpath.c** — Join method path generation (nested loop, merge, hash)
- **src/backend/optimizer/path/costsize.c** — Cost estimation for Paths
- **src/backend/optimizer/path/joinrels.c** — Join relation enumeration using dynamic programming

#### Phase 2: Plan Creation (Selection)
- **src/backend/optimizer/plan/planner.c** — planner() and standard_planner() orchestrate optimization
- **src/backend/opti
